title="Kanji">Kanji</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">大豆<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup></span></span></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/Kana" title="Kana">Kana</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">ダイズ</span></span></td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="infobox-subbox collapsible collapsed" style="display:inline-table; text-align: left;"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above" colspan="2" style="font-size: 100%; text-align: left; background-color: #f9ffbc;">Transcriptions</th></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Japanese" title="Romanization of Japanese">Romanization</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><span lang="ja-Latn" style="font-style: normal">daizu</span></span></td></tr></tbody></table></td><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Soybeans_01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="5184" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Soybeans_01.jpg/220px-Soybeans_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Soybeans_01.jpg/330px-Soybeans_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Soybeans_01.jpg/440px-Soybeans_01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Soybeans_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Soybeans in a supermarket in China</div></div></div>
<p>The <b>soybean</b>, <b>soy bean</b>, or <b>soya bean</b> (<i>Glycine max</i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> is a species of <a href="/wiki/Legume" title="Legume">legume</a> native to <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a>, widely grown for its edible <a href="/wiki/Bean" title="Bean">bean</a>, which has numerous uses.
</p><p>Traditional unfermented food uses of soybeans include <a href="/wiki/Soy_milk" title="Soy milk">soy milk</a>, from which <a href="/wiki/Tofu" title="Tofu">tofu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tofu_skin" title="Tofu skin">tofu skin</a> are made. Fermented soy foods include <a href="/wiki/Soy_sauce" title="Soy sauce">soy sauce</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fermented_bean_paste" title="Fermented bean paste">fermented bean paste</a>, <a href="/wiki/Natt%C5%8D" title="Nattō">nattō</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tempeh" title="Tempeh">tempeh</a>. Fat-free (defatted) soybean meal is a significant and cheap source of protein for animal feeds and many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TV_dinner" title="TV dinner">packaged meals</a>. For example, soybean products, such as <a href="/wiki/Textured_vegetable_protein" title="Textured vegetable protein">textured vegetable protein</a> (TVP), are ingredients in many meat and <a href="/wiki/Dairy" title="Dairy">dairy</a> substitutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Riaz2006_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Riaz2006-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Soybeans contain significant amounts of <a href="/wiki/Phytic_acid" title="Phytic acid">phytic acid</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dietary_minerals" title="Dietary minerals">dietary minerals</a> and <a href="/wiki/B_vitamins" title="B vitamins">B vitamins</a>. <a href="/wiki/Soybean_oil" title="Soybean oil">Soy vegetable oil</a>, used in food and industrial applications, is another product of processing the soybean crop. Soybean is the most important protein source for feed farm animals (that in turn yields animal protein for human consumption).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>The word "soy" originated as a corruption of the <a href="/wiki/Cantonese" title="Cantonese">Cantonese</a> or Japanese names for <a href="/wiki/Soy_sauce" title="Soy sauce">soy sauce</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">豉油</span>; <a href="/wiki/Jyutping" title="Jyutping">Jyutping</a>: <i><span lang="yue-Latn-jyutping">si6jau4</span></i>; <a href="/wiki/Yale_romanization_of_Cantonese" title="Yale romanization of Cantonese">Cantonese Yale</a>: <i><span lang="yue-Latn">sihyàuh</span></i>) <span style="font-weight: normal">(<a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a>: <span lang="ja">醤油</span>, <i>shōyu</i>)</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>The etymology of the genus, <i>Glycine</i>, comes from <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Linnaeus</a>. When naming the genus, Linnaeus observed that one of the species within the genus had a sweet root. Based on the sweetness, the Greek word for sweet, glykós, was Latinized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-6">[6]</a></sup> The genus name is not related to the amino acid <a href="/wiki/Glycine" title="Glycine">glycine</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Soybeanvarieties.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2700" data-file-width="1790" decoding="async" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Soybeanvarieties.jpg/170px-Soybeanvarieties.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Soybeanvarieties.jpg/255px-Soybeanvarieties.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Soybeanvarieties.jpg/340px-Soybeanvarieties.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Soybeanvarieties.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Varieties of soybeans are used for many purposes.</div></div></div>
<p>The genus <i>Glycine</i> is divided into two subgenera, <i>Glycine</i> and <i>Soja</i>. The subgenus <i>Soja</i> (Moench) F.J. Herm. includes the cultivated soybean, <i>Glycine max</i> (L.) Merr., and the wild soybean, <i><a href="/wiki/Glycine_soja" title="Glycine soja">Glycine soja</a></i> Sieb. &amp; Zucc. Both species are <a href="/wiki/Annual_plant" title="Annual plant">annuals</a>. <i>Glycine soja</i> is the wild ancestor of <i>Glycine max</i>, and grows wild in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Glycine_(plant)" title="Glycine (plant)">subgenus <i>Glycine</i></a> consists of at least 25 wild <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perennial" title="Perennial">perennial</a> species: for example, <i>Glycine canescens</i> F.J. Herm. and <i>G. tomentella</i> Hayata, both found in Australia and Papua New Guinea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Perennial soybean (<i><a href="/wiki/Neonotonia_wightii" title="Neonotonia wightii">Neonotonia wightii</a></i>) originated in Africa and is now a widespread pasture crop in the tropics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>Like some other crops of long domestication, the relationship of the modern soybean to wild-growing species can no longer be traced with any degree of certainty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> It is a <a href="/wiki/Cultigen" title="Cultigen">cultural variety</a> with a very large number of cultivars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output <p>Like most plants, soybeans grow in distinct <a href="/wiki/Plant_morphology" title="Plant morphology">morphological</a> stages as they develop from seeds into fully mature plant.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Germination">Germination</span></h3>
<p>The first stage of growth is <a href="/wiki/Germination" title="Germination">germination</a>, a method which first becomes apparent as a seed's <a href="/wiki/Radicle" title="Radicle">radicle</a> emerges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MP197Chapter2_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-MP197Chapter2-15">[15]</a></sup> This is the first stage of root growth and occurs within the first 48 hours under ideal growing conditions. The first <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthetic</a> structures, the <a href="/wiki/Cotyledon" title="Cotyledon">cotyledons</a>, develop from the <a href="/wiki/Hypocotyl" title="Hypocotyl">hypocotyl</a>, the first plant structure to emerge from the soil. These cotyledons both act as leaves and as a source of nutrients for the immature plant, providing the seedling nutrition for its first 7 to 10 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MP197Chapter2_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-MP197Chapter2-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glycine_max_003.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1292" data-file-width="1722" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Glycine_max_003.JPG/200px-Glycine_max_003.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Glycine_max_003.JPG/300px-Glycine_max_003.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Glycine_max_003.JPG/400px-Glycine_max_003.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Glycine_max_003.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Soya bean fruit</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Maturation">Maturation</span></h3>
<p>The first true leaves develop as a pair of <a href="/wiki/Leaf#Divisions_of_the_blade" title="Leaf">single blades</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MP197Chapter2_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-MP197Chapter2-15">[15]</a></sup> Subsequent to this first pair, mature <a href="/wiki/Plant_stem" title="Plant stem">nodes</a> form compound leaves with three blades. Mature <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trifoliolate" title="Trifoliolate">trifoliolate</a> leaves, having three to four <a href="/wiki/Leaflet_(botany)" title="Leaflet (botany)">leaflets</a> per leaf, are often between <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1020198016">.mw-parser-output .frac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .frac .num,.mw-parser-output .frac .den{font-size:80%;line-height:0;vertical-align:super}.mw-parser-output .frac .den{vertical-align:sub}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style>6 and 15 cm (<span class="frac" role="math">2<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="num">1</span>⁄<span class="den">2</span></span> and 6 in) long and 2 and 7 cm (1 and 3 in) broad. Under ideal conditions, stem growth continues, producing new nodes every four days. Before flowering, roots can grow <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/>2 cm (<span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">3</span>⁄<span class="den">4</span></span> in) per day. If <a href="/wiki/Rhizobia" title="Rhizobia">rhizobia</a> are present, <a href="/wiki/Root_nodule" title="Root nodule">root nodulation</a> begins by the time the third node appears. Nodulation typically continues for 8 weeks before the <a href="/wiki/Rhizobia#Infection_and_signal_exchange" title="Rhizobia">symbiotic infection</a> process stabilizes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MP197Chapter2_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-MP197Chapter2-15">[15]</a></sup> The final characteristics of a soybean plant are variable, with factors such as genetics, soil quality, and climate affecting its form; however, fully mature soybean plants are generally between 50 and 125 cm (20 and 50 in) in height<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MP197Chapter19_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-MP197Chapter19-16">[16]</a></sup> and have rooting depths between 75 and 150 cm (30 and 60 in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flowering">Flowering</span></h3>
<p>Flowering is <a href="/wiki/Photoperiodism" title="Photoperiodism">triggered by day length</a>, often beginning once days become shorter than 12.8 hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MP197Chapter2_15-4"><a href="#cite_note-MP197Chapter2-15">[15]</a></sup> This trait is highly variable however, with different <a href="/wiki/Variety_(botany)" title="Variety (botany)">varieties</a> reacting differently to changing day length.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Soybeans form inconspicuous, self-fertile flowers which are borne in the <a href="/wiki/Leaf#Large-scale_features_(leaf_morphology)" title="Leaf">axil</a> of the leaf and are white, pink or purple. Though they do not require pollination, they are attractive to bees, because they produce nectar that is high in sugar content.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Depending on the soybean variety, node growth may cease once flowering begins. Strains that continue nodal development after flowering are termed "<a href="/wiki/Indeterminate_growth" title="Indeterminate growth">indeterminates</a>" and are best suited to climates with longer growing seasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MP197Chapter2_15-5"><a href="#cite_note-MP197Chapter2-15">[15]</a></sup> Often soybeans drop their leaves before the seeds are fully mature.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Soybean_flowers.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="96" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Soybean_flowers.png/120px-Soybean_flowers.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Soybean_flowers.png/180px-Soybean_flowers.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Soybean_flowers.png/240px-Soybean_flowers.png 2x" width="120"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Soybean_flowers.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Small, purple soybean flowers</div></div></div>
<p>The fruit is a hairy <a href="/wiki/Legume" title="Legume">pod</a> that grows in clusters of three to five, each pod is 3–8 cm (1–3 in) long and usually contains two to four (rarely more) <a href="/wiki/Seed" title="Seed">seeds</a> 5–11 mm in diameter. Soybean seeds come in a wide variety of sizes and <a href="/wiki/Husk" title="Husk">hull</a> colors such as black, brown, yellow, and green.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MP197Chapter19_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-MP197Chapter19-16">[16]</a></sup> Variegated and bicolored seed coats are also common.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Seed_resilience">Seed resilience</span></h3>
<p>The hull of the mature bean is hard, water-resistant, and protects the <a href="/wiki/Cotyledon" title="Cotyledon">cotyledon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hypocotyl" title="Hypocotyl">hypocotyl</a> (or "germ") from damage. If the seed coat is cracked, the seed will not <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Germinate" title="Germinate">germinate</a>. The scar, visible on the seed coat, is called the <a href="/wiki/Hilum_(biology)" title="Hilum (biology)">hilum</a> (colors include black, brown, buff, gray and yellow) and at one end of the hilum is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Micropyle_(botany)" title="Micropyle (botany)">micropyle</a>, or small opening in the seed coat which can allow the absorption of water for sprouting.
</p><p>Some seeds such as soybeans containing very high levels of <a href="/wiki/Soy_protein" title="Soy protein">protein</a> can undergo <a href="/wiki/Desiccation" title="Desiccation">desiccation</a>, yet survive and revive after water absorption. <a href="/wiki/A._Carl_Leopold" title="A. Carl Leopold">A. Carl Leopold</a> began studying this capability at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boyce_Thompson_Institute_for_Plant_Research" title="Boyce Thompson Institute for Plant Research">Boyce Thompson Institute for Plant Research</a> at <a href="/wiki/Cornell_University" title="Cornell University">Cornell University</a> in the mid-1980s. He found soybeans and corn to have a range of soluble <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a> protecting the seed's cell viability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Patents were awarded to him in the early 1990s on techniques for protecting biological membranes and proteins in the dry state.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nitrogen-fixing_ability">Nitrogen-fixing ability</span></h3>
<p>Like many legumes, soybeans can <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_fixation" title="Nitrogen fixation">fix atmospheric nitrogen</a>, due to the presence of <a href="/wiki/Symbiosis" title="Symbiosis">symbiotic</a> bacteria from the <a href="/wiki/Rhizobia" title="Rhizobia">Rhizobia</a> group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_composition">Chemical composition</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-More_citations_needed_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">July 2021</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Together, <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soybean_oil" title="Soybean oil">soybean oil</a> content account for 56% of dry soybeans by weight (36% protein and 20% <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fat</a>, table). The remainder consists of 30% <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a>, 9% water and 5% <a href="/wiki/Ash_(analytical_chemistry)" title="Ash (analytical chemistry)">ash</a> (table). Soybeans comprise approximately 8% seed coat or hull, 90% <a href="/wiki/Cotyledon" title="Cotyledon">cotyledons</a> and 2% <a href="/wiki/Hypocotyl" title="Hypocotyl">hypocotyl</a> axis or germ.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>

</td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" </td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="padding-left:0.65em;line-height:1.1em;font-weight:normal;padding-right:0.25em;"><b style="margin-left:-0.65em">Other constituents</b></th><td class="infobox-data" style="vertical-align:middle;padding-left:0.65em;line-height:1.1em;"><b>Quantity</b></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="padding-left:0.65em;line-height:1.1em;font-weight:normal;padding-right:0.25em;">Water</th><td class="infobox-data" style="vertical-align:middle;padding-left:0.65em;line-height:1.1em;">8.54 g</td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="padding-left:0.65em;line-height:1.1em;font-weight:normal;padding-right:0.25em;"><a href="/wiki/Cholesterol" title="Cholesterol">Cholesterol</a></th><td class="infobox-data" style="vertical-align:middle;padding-left:0.65em;line-height:1.1em;">0 mg</td></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">
</td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2"><hr/><div class="wrap" style="padding:0.3em;line-height:1.2em;"><a class="external text" href="https://fdc.nal.usda.gov/fdc-app.html#/food-details/174270/nutrients" rel="nofollow">Link to USDA FoodData Central Entry</a></div></td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2"><div class="plainlist" style="background:#e0e0e0;padding:0.15em;line-height:1.25em;">

<p>A 100-gram reference quantity of raw soybeans supplies 1,866 kilojoules (446 kilocalories) of <a href="/wiki/Food_energy" title="Food energy">food energy</a> and are 9% water, 30% <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a>, 20% total <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fat</a> and 36% <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> (table).
</p><p>Soybeans are a rich source of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Essential_nutrient" title="Essential nutrient">essential nutrients</a>, providing in a 100-gram serving (raw, for reference) high contents of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daily_Value" title="Daily Value">Daily Value</a> (DV) especially for protein (36% DV), <a href="/wiki/Dietary_fiber" title="Dietary fiber">dietary fiber</a> (37%), <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> (121%), <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a> (120%), <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a> (101%) and several <a href="/wiki/B_vitamins" title="B vitamins">B vitamins</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Folate" title="Folate">folate</a> (94%) (table). High contents also exist for <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_K" title="Vitamin K">vitamin K</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a> and <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a> (table).
</p><p>For human consumption, soybeans must be cooked with "wet" heat to destroy the <a href="/wiki/Trypsin_inhibitor" title="Trypsin inhibitor">trypsin inhibitors</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Serine_protease_inhibitor" title="Serine protease inhibitor">serine protease inhibitors</a>). Raw soybeans, including the immature green form, are toxic to all <a href="/wiki/Monogastric" title="Monogastric">monogastric</a> animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Protein">Protein</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Complete_protein" title="Complete protein">Complete protein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protein_quality" title="Protein quality">Protein quality</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Soy_protein" title="Soy protein">Soy protein</a></div>
<p>Most soy protein is a relatively heat-stable storage protein. This heat stability enables soy food products requiring high temperature cooking, such as <a href="/wiki/Tofu" title="Tofu">tofu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soy_milk" title="Soy milk">soy milk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Textured_vegetable_protein" title="Textured vegetable protein">textured vegetable protein</a> (soy flour) to be made.  <a href="/wiki/Soy_protein" title="Soy protein">Soy protein</a> is essentially identical to the protein of other <a href="/wiki/Legume" title="Legume">legume</a> seeds and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pulses" title="Pulses">pulses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>Soy is a good source of protein for vegetarians and <a href="/wiki/Veganism" title="Veganism">vegans</a> or for people who want to reduce the amount of meat they eat, according to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration_(United_States)" title="Food and Drug Administration (United States)">US Food and Drug Administration</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fda-chd_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-fda-chd-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Soy protein products can be good substitutes for animal products because, unlike some other beans, soy offers a 'complete' protein profile. ... Soy protein products can replace animal-based foods—which also have complete proteins but tend to contain more fat, especially saturated fat—without requiring major adjustments elsewhere in the diet.</p></blockquote>
<p>Although soybeans have high protein content, soybeans also contain high levels of <a href="/wiki/Protease_inhibitor_(biology)" title="Protease inhibitor (biology)">protease inhibitors</a>, which can prevent digestion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16001874_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16001874-27">[27]</a></sup> Protease inhibitors are reduced by cooking soybeans, and are present in low levels in soy products such as <a href="/wiki/Tofu" title="Tofu">tofu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soy_milk" title="Soy milk">soy milk</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16001874_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16001874-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PDCAAS" title="PDCAAS">Protein Digestibility Corrected Amino Acid Score</a> (PDCAAS) of soy protein is the nutritional equivalent of meat, eggs, and <a href="/wiki/Casein" title="Casein">casein</a> for human growth and health. Soybean protein isolate has a <a href="/wiki/Biological_value" title="Biological value">biological value</a> of 74, whole soybeans 96, soybean milk 91, and eggs 97.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1991Report_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-1991Report-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Graded_Seed.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3120" data-file-width="4128" decoding="async" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Graded_Seed.jpg/200px-Graded_Seed.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Graded_Seed.jpg/300px-Graded_Seed.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Graded_Seed.jpg/400px-Graded_Seed.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Graded_Seed.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Graded seed</div></div></div>
<p>All <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spermatophytes" title="Spermatophytes">spermatophytes</a>, except for the family of grasses and cereals (<a href="/wiki/Poaceae" title="Poaceae">Poaceae</a>), contain 7S (vicilin) and 11S (legumin) soy protein-like globulin storage proteins; or only one of these <a href="/wiki/Globulin" title="Globulin">globulin</a> proteins. S denotes <a href="/wiki/Svedberg" title="Svedberg">Svedberg</a>, sedimentation coefficients. Oats and rice are anomalous in that they also contain a majority of soybean-like protein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seed_Proteins_1999_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Seed_Proteins_1999-29">[29]</a></sup> Cocoa, for example, contains the 7S globulin, which contributes to cocoa/chocolate taste and aroma,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> whereas coffee beans (coffee grounds) contain the 11S globulin responsible for coffee's aroma and flavor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Vicilin and legumin proteins belong to the <a href="/wiki/Cupin_superfamily" title="Cupin superfamily">cupin superfamily</a>, a large family of functionally diverse proteins that have a common origin and whose evolution can be followed from bacteria to eukaryotes including animals and higher plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>2S <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Albumins" title="Albumins">albumins</a> form a major group of homologous storage proteins in many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dicot" title="Dicot">dicot</a> species and in some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monocots" title="Monocots">monocots</a> but not in grasses (cereals).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-36">[36]</a></sup> Soybeans contain a small but significant 2S storage protein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seber-et-al-2012_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Seber-et-al-2012-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> 2S albumin are grouped in the <a href="/wiki/Prolamin" title="Prolamin">prolamin</a> superfamily.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Other allergenic proteins included in this 'superfamily' are the non-specific <a href="/wiki/Plant_lipid_transfer_proteins" title="Plant lipid transfer proteins">plant lipid transfer proteins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alpha_amylase_inhibitor" title="Alpha amylase inhibitor">alpha amylase inhibitor</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trypsin_inhibitors" title="Trypsin inhibitors">trypsin inhibitors</a>, and prolamin storage proteins of cereals and grasses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seed_Proteins_1999_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Seed_Proteins_1999-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Peanut" title="Peanut">Peanuts</a>, for instance, contain 20% 2S albumin but only 6% 7S globulin and 74% 11S.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-36">[36]</a></sup> It is the high 2S albumin and low 7S globulin that is responsible for the relatively low lysine content of peanut protein compared to soy protein.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbohydrates">Carbohydrates</span></h4>
<p>The principal soluble <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a> of mature soybeans are the disaccharide <a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose">sucrose</a> (range 2.5–8.2%), the trisaccharide <a href="/wiki/Raffinose" title="Raffinose">raffinose</a> (0.1–1.0%) composed of one sucrose molecule connected to one molecule of <a href="/wiki/Galactose" title="Galactose">galactose</a>, and the tetrasaccharide <a href="/wiki/Stachyose" title="Stachyose">stachyose</a> (1.4 to 4.1%) composed of one sucrose connected to two molecules of galactose.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="removed citation associated with a predatory publisher (December 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>  While the <a href="/wiki/Oligosaccharide" title="Oligosaccharide">oligosaccharides</a> raffinose and stachyose protect the viability of the soybean seed from desiccation (see above section on physical characteristics) they are not digestible sugars, so contribute to <a href="/wiki/Flatulence" title="Flatulence">flatulence</a> and abdominal discomfort in humans and other <a href="/wiki/Monogastric" title="Monogastric">monogastric</a> animals, comparable to the disaccharide <a href="/wiki/Trehalose" title="Trehalose">trehalose</a>. Undigested oligosaccharides are broken down in the intestine by native microbes, producing gases such as <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>.
</p><p>Since soluble soy carbohydrates are found in the whey and are broken down during fermentation, soy concentrate, soy protein isolates, tofu, soy sauce, and sprouted soybeans are without flatus activity. On the other hand, there may be some beneficial effects to ingesting oligosaccharides such as raffinose and stachyose, namely, encouraging indigenous <a href="/wiki/Bifidobacterium" title="Bifidobacterium">bifidobacteria</a> in the colon against putrefactive bacteria.
</p><p>The insoluble carbohydrates in soybeans consist of the complex polysaccharides <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hemicellulose" title="Hemicellulose">hemicellulose</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pectin" title="Pectin">pectin</a>. The majority of soybean carbohydrates can be classed as belonging to <a href="/wiki/Dietary_fiber" title="Dietary fiber">dietary fiber</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fats">Fats</span></h4>
<p>Raw soybeans are 20% fat, including <a href="/wiki/Saturated_fat" title="Saturated fat">saturated fat</a> (3%), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monounsaturated_fat" title="Monounsaturated fat">monounsaturated fat</a> (4%) and polyunsaturated fat, mainly as <a href="/wiki/Linoleic_acid" title="Linoleic acid">linoleic acid</a> (table).
</p><p>Within <a href="/wiki/Soybean_oil" title="Soybean oil">soybean oil</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipid</a> portion of the seed is contained four <a href="/wiki/Phytosterol" title="Phytosterol">phytosterols</a>: <a href="/wiki/Stigmasterol" title="Stigmasterol">stigmasterol</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sitosterol" title="Sitosterol">sitosterol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Campesterol" title="Campesterol">campesterol</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Brassicasterol" title="Brassicasterol">brassicasterol</a> accounting for about 2.5% of the lipid fraction; and which can be converted into <a href="/wiki/Steroid_hormone" title="Steroid hormone">steroid hormones</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="removed citation associated with a predatory publisher (December 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>  Additionally soybeans are a rich source of <a href="/wiki/Sphingolipid" title="Sphingolipid">sphingolipids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_constituents">Other constituents</span></h4>
<p>Soy contains <a href="/wiki/Isoflavone" title="Isoflavone">isoflavones</a>—<a href="/wiki/Polyphenol" title="Polyphenol">polyphenolic</a> compounds, produced by legumes including peanuts and <a href="/wiki/Chickpea" title="Chickpea">chickpeas</a>. Isoflavones are closely related to <a href="/wiki/Flavonoid" title="Flavonoid">flavonoids</a> found in other plants, vegetables and flowers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-lpi-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>Soy contains the <a href="/wiki/Phytoestrogen" title="Phytoestrogen">phytoestrogen</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coumestans" title="Coumestans">coumestans</a>, also are found in beans and split-peas, with the best sources being alfalfa, clover, and soybean sprouts. <a href="/wiki/Coumestrol" title="Coumestrol">Coumestrol</a>, an isoflavone <a href="/wiki/Coumarin" title="Coumarin">coumarin</a> derivative, is the only coumestan in foods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saponins" title="Saponins">Saponins</a>, a class of natural <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surfactants" title="Surfactants">surfactants</a> (soaps), are sterols that are present in small amounts in various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Whole_food" title="Whole food">plant foods</a>, including soybeans, other <a href="/wiki/Legume" title="Legume">legumes</a>, and cereals, such as oats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hu_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hu-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparison_to_other_major_staple_foods">Comparison to other major staple foods</span></h3>
<p>The following table shows the nutrient content of green soybean and other major staple foods, each in respective raw form on a <a href="/wiki/Dry_matter#Dry_matter_basis" title="Dry matter">dry weight basis</a> to account for their different water contents. Raw soybeans, however, are not edible and cannot be digested. These must be sprouted, or prepared and cooked for human consumption. In sprouted and cooked form, the relative nutritional and anti-nutritional contents of each of these grains is remarkably different from that of raw form of these grains reported in this table. The nutritional value of soybean and each cooked staple depends on the processing and the method of cooking: boiling, frying, roasting, baking, etc.

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultivation">Cultivation</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AreialSeedingPlane.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2790" data-file-width="1873" decoding="async" height="253" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/AreialSeedingPlane.jpg/170px-AreialSeedingPlane.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/AreialSeedingPlane.jpg/255px-AreialSeedingPlane.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/AreialSeedingPlane.jpg/340px-AreialSeedingPlane.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AreialSeedingPlane.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Biplane" title="Biplane">Biplane</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cropduster" title="Cropduster">cropduster</a> over US soybean field</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-More_citations_needed_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">July 2021</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">Uses</span></h3>
<p>During World War II, soybeans became important in both North America and Europe chiefly as substitutes for other protein foods and as a source of edible oil. During the war, the soybean was discovered as <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizer</a> due to <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_fixation" title="Nitrogen fixation">nitrogen fixation</a> by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Agriculture" title="United States Department of Agriculture">United States Department of Agriculture</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conditions">Conditions</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sembrado_de_soja_en_argentina.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Sembrado_de_soja_en_argentina.jpg/200px-Sembrado_de_soja_en_argentina.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Sembrado_de_soja_en_argentina.jpg/300px-Sembrado_de_soja_en_argentina.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Sembrado_de_soja_en_argentina.jpg/400px-Sembrado_de_soja_en_argentina.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sembrado_de_soja_en_argentina.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Soya fields in Argentina</div></div></div>
<p>Cultivation is successful in climates with hot summers, with optimum growing conditions in mean temperatures of 20 to 30 °C (70 to 85 °F); temperatures of below 20 °C (70 °F) and over 40 °C (105 °F) stunt growth significantly. They can grow in a wide range of soils, with optimum growth in moist <a href="/wiki/Alluvium" title="Alluvium">alluvial soils</a> with good organic content. Soybeans, like most legumes, perform <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_fixation" title="Nitrogen fixation">nitrogen fixation</a> by establishing a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symbiotic" title="Symbiotic">symbiotic</a> relationship with the bacterium <i><a href="/wiki/Bradyrhizobium_japonicum" title="Bradyrhizobium japonicum">Bradyrhizobium japonicum</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Synonym_(taxonomy)" title="Synonym (taxonomy)">syn.</a> <i>Rhizobium japonicum</i>; Jordan 1982). This ability to fix nitrogen allows farmers to reduce nitrogen <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizer</a> use and increase yields when growing other crops in <a href="/wiki/Crop_rotation" title="Crop rotation">rotation</a> with soy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> There may be some trade-offs, however, in the long-term abundance of <a href="/wiki/Soil_organic_matter" title="Soil organic matter">organic material in soils</a> where soy and other crops (for example, <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">corn</a>) are grown in rotation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> For best results, though, an inoculum of the correct strain of bacteria should be mixed with the soybean (or any legume) seed before planting. Modern crop <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">cultivars</a> generally reach a height of around 1 m (3 ft), and take 80–120 days from sowing to harvesting.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Soils">Soils</span></h3>
<p>Soil scientists <a href="/wiki/Edson_Lobato" title="Edson Lobato">Edson Lobato</a> (Brazil), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Andrew_McClung" title="Andrew McClung">Andrew McClung</a> (U.S.), and <a href="/wiki/Alysson_Paolinelli" title="Alysson Paolinelli">Alysson Paolinelli</a> (Brazil) were awarded the 2006 <a href="/wiki/World_Food_Prize" title="World Food Prize">World Food Prize</a> for transforming the ecologically biodiverse savannah of the <a href="/wiki/Cerrado" title="Cerrado">Cerrado</a> region of Brazil into highly productive cropland that could grow profitable soybeans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Soybean_rust.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Soybean_rust.jpg/200px-Soybean_rust.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Soybean_rust.jpg/300px-Soybean_rust.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Soybean_rust.jpg/400px-Soybean_rust.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Soybean_rust.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Soybean rust</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contamination_concerns">Contamination concerns</span></h3>
<p>Human <a href="/wiki/Sewage_sludge" title="Sewage sludge">sewage sludge</a> can be used as fertilizer to grow soybeans. Soybeans grown in sewage sludge likely contain elevated concentrations of metals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pests">Pests</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_soybean_diseases" title="List of soybean diseases">List of soybean diseases</a></div>
<p>Soybean plants are vulnerable to a wide range of bacterial diseases, fungal diseases, viral diseases and parasites.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bacteria">Bacteria</span></h4>
<p>The primary bacterial diseases include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bacterial_blight" title="Bacterial blight">bacterial blight</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bacterial_pustule" title="Bacterial pustule">bacterial pustule</a> and <a href="/wiki/Downy_mildew" title="Downy mildew">downy mildew</a> affecting the soybean plant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Animals">Animals</span></h4>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Nematodes">Nematodes</span></h5>
<p><a href="/wiki/Soybean_cyst_nematode" title="Soybean cyst nematode">Soybean cyst nematode</a> is the worst pest of soybean in the US. Losses of 30%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Missouri_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Missouri-57">[57]</a></sup> or 40%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tylka-40-sympt_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tylka-40-sympt-58">[RM 1]</a></sup> are common even without symptoms.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Arthropods">Arthropods</span></h5>
<h6><span class="mw-headline" id="Insects">Insects</span></h6>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Helicoverpa_zea" title="Helicoverpa zea">corn earworm moth and bollworm</a> is a common and destructive pest of soybean growth in Virginia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Vertebrates">Vertebrates</span></h5>
<h6><span class="mw-headline" id="Mammals">Mammals</span></h6>
<p>Soybeans are consumed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Whitetail_deer" title="Whitetail deer">whitetail deer</a> which may damage soybean plants through feeding, trampling and bedding, reducing <a href="/wiki/Crop_yield" title="Crop yield">crop yields</a> by as much as 15%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ag_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-ag-60">[59]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Groundhog" title="Groundhog">Groundhogs</a> are also a common pest in soybean fields, living in burrows underground and feeding nearby. One den of groundhogs can consume a tenth to a quarter of an acre of soybeans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brant_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-brant-61">[60]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Animal_repellent" title="Animal repellent">Chemical repellents</a> or <a href="/wiki/Firearm" title="Firearm">firearms</a> are effective for controlling pests in soybean fields.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ag_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-ag-60">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brant_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-brant-61">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Fungus"></span><span class="anchor" id="Funguses"></span>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fungi">Fungi</span></h4>
<p>Soybeans suffer from <i><a href="/wiki/Pythium_spinosum" title="Pythium spinosum">Pythium spinosum</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Arkansas" title="Arkansas">Arkansas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indiana" title="Indiana">Indiana</a> (<a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>), and <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P-spinosum-US-Nat-Fung-Coll_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-P-spinosum-US-Nat-Fung-Coll-62">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultivars">Cultivars</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Disease_resistant_cultivars">Disease resistant cultivars</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Plant_disease_resistance" title="Plant disease resistance">Resistant varieties</a> are available.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="PI_88788">PI 88788</span></h5>
<p>The vast majority of cultivars in the US have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soybean_cyst_nematode_resistance" title="Soybean cyst nematode resistance">SCN resistance</a>, but rely on <a href="/wiki/Soybean_cyst_nematode#PI_88788" title="Soybean cyst nematode">only one breeding line (PI 88788)</a> as their sole source of resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PI-88788_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-PI-88788-63">[RM 2]</a></sup> (The resistance genes provided by PI 88788, Peking<span class="anchor" id="Peking"></span>, and PI 90763<span class="anchor" id="PI_90763"></span> were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genome_mapping" title="Genome mapping">characterized</a> in 1997.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Concibido-et-al-1997_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Concibido-et-al-1997-64">[62]</a></sup> As a result, for example, in 2012 only 18 cultivars out of 807 recommended by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iowa_State_University_Extension" title="Iowa State University Extension">Iowa State University Extension</a> had any ancestry outside of PI 88788. By 2020 the situation was still about the same: Of 849 there were 810 with some ancestry from PI 88788,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iowa-other-resist_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Iowa-other-resist-65">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iowa-resist_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Iowa-resist-66">[64]</a></sup> 35 from Peking, and only 2 from PI 89772. (On the question of exclusively PI 88788 ancestry, that number was not available for 2020.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iowa-resist_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-Iowa-resist-66">[64]</a></sup> That was speculated to be in 2012<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-overcome-resis_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-overcome-resis-67">[RM 3]</a></sup>—and was clearly by 2020<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iowa-other-resist_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-Iowa-other-resist-65">[63]</a></sup>—producing SCN populations that are virulent on PI 88788.
</p>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PRODUCTION_OF_SOYBEANS_(2018).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="263" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/PRODUCTION_OF_SOYBEANS_%282018%29.svg/220px-PRODUCTION_OF_SOYBEANS_%282018%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/PRODUCTION_OF_SOYBEANS_%282018%29.svg/330px-PRODUCTION_OF_SOYBEANS_%282018%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/PRODUCTION_OF_SOYBEANS_%282018%29.svg/440px-PRODUCTION_OF_SOYBEANS_%282018%29.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PRODUCTION_OF_SOYBEANS_(2018).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Production of soybeans (2018)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[66]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In 2020, world production of soybeans was over 353 million tonnes, led by Brazil and the United States combined with 66% of the total (table). Production has dramatically increased across the globe since the 1960s, but particularly in South America after a cultivar that grew well in low latitudes was developed in the 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[67]</a></sup> The rapid growth of the industry has been primarily fueled by large increases in <a href="/wiki/Western_pattern_diet" title="Western pattern diet">worldwide demand for meat</a> products, particularly in developing countries like China, which alone accounts for more than 60% of imports.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_issues">Environmental issues</span></h3>
<p>In spite of the Amazon "Soy Moratorium", soy production continues to play a significant role in <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a> when its indirect impacts are taken into account, as land used to grow soy continues to increase. This land either comes from <a href="/wiki/Pasture" title="Pasture">pasture</a> land (which increasingly supplants forested areas), or areas outside the Amazon not covered by the moratorium, such as the <a href="/wiki/Cerrado" title="Cerrado">Cerrado</a> region. Roughly one-fifth of deforestation can be attributed to expanding land use to produce oilseeds, primarily for soy and <a href="/wiki/Palm_oil" title="Palm oil">palm oil</a>, whereas the expansion of <a href="/wiki/Beef" title="Beef">beef</a> production accounts for 41%. The main driver of deforestation is the global demand for meat, which in turn requires huge tracts of land to grow feed crops for livestock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[69]</a></sup> Around 80% of the global soybean crop is used to feed livestock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-More_citations_needed_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">July 2021</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Soybeans were a crucial crop in East Asia long before written records began.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[71]</a></sup> The origin of soy bean cultivation remains scientifically debated. The closest living relative of the soybean is <i><a href="/wiki/Glycine_soja" title="Glycine soja">Glycine soja</a></i> (previously called <i>G. ussuriensis</i>), a legume native to central China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-75">[72]</a></sup> There is evidence for soybean domestication between 7000 and 6600 BC in China, between 5000 and 3000 BC in Japan and 1000 BC in Korea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lee-et-al-2011_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lee-et-al-2011-76">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first unambiguously domesticated, <a href="/wiki/Cultigen" title="Cultigen">cultigen</a>-sized soybean was discovered in Korea at the <a href="/wiki/Mumun_pottery_period" title="Mumun pottery period">Mumun</a>-period Daundong site.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lee-et-al-2011_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lee-et-al-2011-76">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stark-2017_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stark-2017-77">[74]</a></sup> Prior to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(food)" title="Fermentation (food)">fermented</a> products such as fermented black soybeans (<i><a href="/wiki/Douchi" title="Douchi">douchi</a></i>), <i>jiang</i> (Chinese miso), <a href="/wiki/Soy_sauce" title="Soy sauce">soy sauce</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tempeh" title="Tempeh">tempeh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Natt%C5%8D" title="Nattō">nattō</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Miso" title="Miso">miso</a>, soy was considered sacred for its beneficial effects in <a href="/wiki/Crop_rotation" title="Crop rotation">crop rotation</a>, and it was eaten by itself, and as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bean_curd" title="Bean curd">bean curd</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soy_milk" title="Soy milk">soy milk</a>.
</p><p>Soybeans were introduced to <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a> in <a href="/wiki/Malay_Archipelago" title="Malay Archipelago">Malay Archipelago</a> circa 13th century or probably earlier. By the 17th century through their trade with Far East, soybeans and its products were traded by European traders (Portuguese, Spanish, and Dutch) in Asia, and reached Indian Subcontinent by this period.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> By the 18th century, soybeans were introduced to the Americas and Europe from China. Soy was introduced to Africa from China in the late 19th century, and is now widespread across the continent.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="East_Asia">East Asia</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leiden_University_Library_-_Seikei_Zusetsu_vol._18,_page_023_-_%E7%A9%AD%E8%B1%86_-_Glycine_max_(L.)_Merr.,_1804.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="537" decoding="async" height="328" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Leiden_University_Library_-_Seikei_Zusetsu_vol._18%2C_page_023_-_%E7%A9%AD%E8%B1%86_-_Glycine_max_%28L.%29_Merr.%2C_1804.jpg/220px-Leiden_University_Library_-_Seikei_Zusetsu_vol._18%2C_page_023_-_%E7%A9%AD%E8%B1%86_-_Glycine_max_%28L.%29_Merr.%2C_1804.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Leiden_University_Library_-_Seikei_Zusetsu_vol._18%2C_page_023_-_%E7%A9%AD%E8%B1%86_-_Glycine_max_%28L.%29_Merr.%2C_1804.jpg/330px-Leiden_University_Library_-_Seikei_Zusetsu_vol._18%2C_page_023_-_%E7%A9%AD%E8%B1%86_-_Glycine_max_%28L.%29_Merr.%2C_1804.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Leiden_University_Library_-_Seikei_Zusetsu_vol._18%2C_page_023_-_%E7%A9%AD%E8%B1%86_-_Glycine_max_%28L.%29_Merr.%2C_1804.jpg/440px-Leiden_University_Library_-_Seikei_Zusetsu_vol._18%2C_page_023_-_%E7%A9%AD%E8%B1%86_-_Glycine_max_%28L.%29_Merr.%2C_1804.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leiden_University_Library_-_Seikei_Zusetsu_vol._18,_page_023_-_%E7%A9%AD%E8%B1%86_-_Glycine_max_(L.)_Merr.,_1804.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Soybean, illustration from the Japanese agricultural encyclopedia <i>Seikei Zusetsu</i> (1804)</div></div></div>
<p>The cultivation of soybeans began in the eastern half of northern China by 2000 BC, but is almost certainly much older.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murphy_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Murphy-78">[75]</a></sup> The earliest documented evidence for the use of Glycine of any kind comes from charred plant remains of wild soybean recovered from Jiahu in Henan province China, a Neolithic site occupied between 9000 and 7800 calendar years ago (cal bp).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lee-et-al-2011_76-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lee-et-al-2011-76">[73]</a></sup> An abundance of archeological charred soybean specimens have been found centered around this region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the ancient Chinese myth, in 2853 BC, the legendary <a href="/wiki/Shennong" title="Shennong">Emperor Shennong</a> of China proclaimed that five plants were sacred: soybeans, rice, wheat, barley, and <a href="/wiki/Millet" title="Millet">millet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[77]</a></sup> Early Chinese records mention that soybeans were a gift from the region of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yangtze_River_delta" title="Yangtze River delta">Yangtze River delta</a> and Southeast China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_Educational_Publishing_p._48_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica_Educational_Publishing_p._48-81">[78]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Great_Soviet_Encyclopedia" title="Great Soviet Encyclopedia">Great Soviet Encyclopedia</a></i> claims soybean cultivation originated in China about 5000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[79]</a></sup> Some scholars suggest that soybean originated in China and was domesticated about 3500 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Siddiqi_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Siddiqi-83">[80]</a></sup> Recent research, however, indicates that seeding of wild forms started early (before 5000 BC) in multiple locations throughout East Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lee-et-al-2011_76-3"><a href="#cite_note-Lee-et-al-2011-76">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>The oldest preserved soybeans resembling modern varieties in size and shape were found in <a href="/wiki/Archaeological_site" title="Archaeological site">archaeological sites</a> in <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a> dated about 1000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_Educational_Publishing_p._48_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica_Educational_Publishing_p._48-81">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stark_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-stark-84">[81]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Radiocarbon_dating" title="Radiocarbon dating">Radiocarbon dating</a> of soybean samples recovered through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flotation_(archaeology)" title="Flotation (archaeology)">flotation</a> during excavations at the Early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mumun" title="Mumun">Mumun</a> period Okbang site in Korea indicated soybean was cultivated as a food crop in around 1000–900 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stark_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-stark-84">[81]</a></sup> Soybeans from the Jōmon period in Japan from 3000 BC<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lee-et-al-2011_76-4"><a href="#cite_note-Lee-et-al-2011-76">[73]</a></sup> are also significantly larger than wild varieties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lee-et-al-2011_76-5"><a href="#cite_note-Lee-et-al-2011-76">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>Soybeans became an important crop by the Zhou Dynasty (c. 1046–256 BC) in China. However, the details of where, when, and under what circumstances soybean developed a close relationship with people are poorly understood. Soybean was unknown in South China before the Han period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lee-et-al-2011_76-6"><a href="#cite_note-Lee-et-al-2011-76">[73]</a></sup> From about the first century AD to the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Discovery" title="Age of Discovery">Age of Discovery</a> (15–16th centuries), soybeans were introduced into across South and Southeast Asia. This spread was due to the establishment of sea and land trade routes. The earliest Japanese textual reference to the soybean is in the classic <i><a href="/wiki/Kojiki" title="Kojiki">Kojiki</a></i> (<i>Records of Ancient Matters</i>), which was completed in AD 712.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Southeast_Asia">Southeast Asia</span></h3>
<p>Soybeans were mentioned as <i>kadêlê</i> (modern <a href="/wiki/Indonesian_language" title="Indonesian language">Indonesian</a> term: <span title="Indonesian-language text"><i lang="id">kedelai</i></span><span class="lang-comment" style="font-style: normal; display: none; color: #33aa33; margin-left: 0.3em;">code: ind promoted to code: id </span>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[83]</a></sup> in an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Javanese" title="Old Javanese">old Javanese</a> manuscript, Serat <a href="/wiki/Sri_Tanjung" title="Sri Tanjung">Sri Tanjung</a>, which dates to 12th- to 13th-century <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Historia_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Historia-87">[84]</a></sup> By the 13th century, the soybean had arrived and cultivated in Indonesia; it probably arrived much earlier however, carried by traders or merchants from Southern China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest known reference to it as "<a href="/wiki/Tempeh" title="Tempeh">tempeh</a>" appeared in 1815 in the <a href="/wiki/Serat_Centhini" title="Serat Centhini">Serat Centhini</a> manuscript.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[86]</a></sup> The development of tempeh fermented soybean cake probably took place earlier, circa 17th century in Java.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indian_subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</span></h3>
<p>By the 1600s, soy sauce spread from southern Japan across the region through the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_India_Company" title="Dutch East India Company">Dutch East India Company</a> (VOC).
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Soya_Bean.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3024" data-file-width="4032" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Soya_Bean.jpg/220px-Soya_Bean.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Soya_Bean.jpg/330px-Soya_Bean.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Soya_Bean.jpg/440px-Soya_Bean.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Soya_Bean.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Soybeans from a high-altitude area of <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Soyabean_field.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3120" data-file-width="4160" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Soyabean_field.jpg/220px-Soyabean_field.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Soyabean_field.jpg/330px-Soyabean_field.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Soyabean_field.jpg/440px-Soyabean_field.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Soyabean_field.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Soyabean field in India</div></div></div>
<p>The soybean probably arrived from southern China, moving southwest into northern parts of Indian subcontinent by this period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Iberia">Iberia</span></h3>
<p>In 1603, "<a href="/wiki/Nippo_Jisho" title="Nippo Jisho">Vocabvlario da Lingoa de Iapam</a>", a famous Japanese-Portuguese dictionary, was compiled and published by Jesuit priests in Nagasaki. It contains short but clear definitions for about 20 words related to soyfoods—the first in any European language.
</p><p>The Luso-Hispanic traders were familiar with soybeans and soybean product through their trade with Far East since at least the 17th century. However, it was not until the late 19th century that the first attempt to cultivate soybeans in the Iberian peninsula was undertaken. In 1880, the soybean was first cultivated in Portugal in the <a href="/wiki/Botanical_Garden_of_the_University_of_Coimbra" title="Botanical Garden of the University of Coimbra">Botanical Gardens at Coimbra</a> (Crespi 1935).
</p><p>In about 1910 in Spain the first attempts at Soybean cultivation were made by the Count of San Bernardo, who cultivated soybeans on his estates at Almillo (in southwest Spain) about 48 miles east-northeast of Seville.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North_America">North America</span></h3>
<p>Soybeans were first introduced to North America from China in 1765, by <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Bowen" title="Samuel Bowen">Samuel Bowen</a>, a former <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a> sailor who had visited China in conjunction with <a href="/wiki/James_Flint_(merchant)" title="James Flint (merchant)">James Flint</a>, the first Englishman legally permitted by the Chinese authorities to learn Chinese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[89]</a></sup> The first "New World" soybean crop was grown on <a href="/wiki/Skidaway_Island,_Georgia" title="Skidaway Island, Georgia">Skidaway Island, Georgia</a>, in 1765 by Henry Yonge from seeds given him by Samuel Bowen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[92]</a></sup> Bowen grew soy near <a href="/wiki/Savannah,_Georgia" title="Savannah, Georgia">Savannah, Georgia</a>, possibly using funds from Flint, and made soy sauce for sale to England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coastalfields_Press_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coastalfields_Press-96">[93]</a></sup> Although soybean was introduced into North America in 1765, for the next 155 years, the crop was grown primarily for <a href="/wiki/Forage" title="Forage">forage</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1831, the first soy product "a few dozen India Soy" [sauce] arrived in Canada. Soybeans were probably first cultivated in Canada by 1855, and definitely in 1895 at Ontario Agricultural College.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>It was not until <a href="/wiki/Lafayette_Mendel" title="Lafayette Mendel">Lafayette Mendel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Burr_Osborne_(chemist)" title="Thomas Burr Osborne (chemist)">Thomas Burr Osborne</a> showed that the nutritional value of soybean seeds could be increased by cooking, moisture or heat, that soy went from a farm animal feed to a human food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>William Morse is considered the "father" of modern soybean agriculture in America. He and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Piper" title="Charles Piper">Charles Piper</a> (Dr. C. V. Piper) took what was an unknown Oriental peasant crop in 1910 and transformed it into a "golden bean" for America, becoming one of America's largest farm crops and its most nutritious.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Soybeans_2021_US_map.pdf"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1125" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Soybeans_2021_US_map.pdf/page1-440px-Soybeans_2021_US_map.pdf.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Soybeans_2021_US_map.pdf/page1-660px-Soybeans_2021_US_map.pdf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Soybeans_2021_US_map.pdf/page1-880px-Soybeans_2021_US_map.pdf.jpg 2x" width="440"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Soybeans_2021_US_map.pdf" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Soybeans planted area 2021 US map by state</div></div></div>
<p>Prior to the 1920 in the US, the soybean was mainly a <a href="/wiki/Forage" title="Forage">forage</a> crop, a source of oil, meal (for feed) and industrial products, with very little used as food. However, it took on an important role after World War I. During the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a>, the drought-stricken (<a href="/wiki/Dust_Bowl" title="Dust Bowl">Dust Bowl</a>) regions of the United States were able to use soy to regenerate their soil because of its nitrogen-fixing properties. Farms were increasing production to meet with government demands, and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Ford" title="Henry Ford">Henry Ford</a> became a promoter of soybeans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-104">[101]</a></sup> In 1931, Ford hired chemists <a href="/wiki/Robert_Boyer_(chemist)" title="Robert Boyer (chemist)">Robert Boyer</a> and Frank Calvert to produce <a href="/wiki/Artificial_silk" title="Artificial silk">artificial silk</a>. They succeeded in making a textile fiber of spun soy protein fibers, hardened or tanned in a <a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde">formaldehyde</a> bath, which was given the name <a href="/wiki/Azlon" title="Azlon">Azlon</a>. It never reached the commercial market. Soybean oil was used by <a href="/wiki/Ford_Motor_Company" title="Ford Motor Company">Ford</a> in <a href="/wiki/Soy_paint" title="Soy paint">paint</a> for the automobiles,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwarcz_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schwarcz-105">[102]</a></sup> as well as a fluid for shock absorbers.
</p><p>Prior to the 1970s, Asian-Americans and Seventh-Day Adventists were essentially the only users of soy foods in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[103]</a></sup> "The soy foods movement began in small pockets of the counterculture, notably the Tennessee commune named simply <a href="/wiki/The_Farm_(Tennessee)" title="The Farm (Tennessee)">The Farm</a>, but by the mid-1970s a vegetarian revival helped it gain momentum and even popular awareness through books such as <a href="/wiki/William_Shurtleff" title="William Shurtleff"><i>The Book of Tofu</i></a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[104]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although practically unseen in 1900, by 2000 soybean plantings covered more than 70 million acres,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[105]</a></sup> second only to corn, and it became America's largest cash crop.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In 2021, 87,195 acres were planted, with the largest acreage in the states of Illinois, Iowa, and Minnesota.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Caribbean_and_West_Indies">Caribbean and West Indies</span></h3>
<p>The soybean arrived in the Caribbean in the form of soy sauce made by Samuel Bowen in Savannah, Georgia, in 1767. It remains only a minor crop there, but its uses for human food are growing steadily.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mediterranean_area">Mediterranean area</span></h3>
<p>The soybean was first cultivated in Italy by 1760 in the Botanical Garden of Turin.
