During the 1780s, it was grown in at least three other botanical gardens in Italy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[108]</a></sup> The first soybean product, soy oil, arrived in <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> during 1909 under <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-112">[109]</a></sup> The first clear cultivation occurred in 1931.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-112">[109]</a></sup> This was also the first time that soybeans were cultivated in Middle East.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_112-2"><a href="#cite_note-:2-112">[109]</a></sup> By 1939, soybeans were cultivated in Greece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span></h3>
<p>Wild soybeans were discovered in northeastern Australia in 1770 by explorers Banks and Solander. In 1804, the first soyfood product ("Fine India Soy" [sauce]) was sold in Sydney. In 1879, the first domesticated soybeans arrived in Australia, a gift of the Minister of the Interior Department, Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Western_Europe">Western Europe</span></h3>
<p>The soybean was first cultivated in France by 1779 (and perhaps as early as 1740). The two key early people and organizations introducing the soybean to France were the Society of Acclimatization (starting in 1855) and Li Yu-ying (from 1910). Li started a large tofu factory, where the first commercial soyfoods in France were made.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Africa">Africa</span></h3>
<p>The soybean first arrived in Africa via Egypt in 1857.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[114]</a></sup> Soya Meme (Baked Soya) is produced in the village called Bame Awudome near <a href="/wiki/Ho,_Ghana" title="Ho, Ghana">Ho</a>, the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Volta_Region" title="Volta Region">Volta Region</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghana</a>, by the <a href="/wiki/Ewe_people" title="Ewe people">Ewe people</a> of Southeastern Ghana and southern Togo.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Central_Europe">Central Europe</span></h3>
<p>In 1873, Professor <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_J._Haberlandt" title="Friedrich J. Haberlandt">Friedrich J. Haberlandt</a> first became interested in soybeans when he obtained the seeds of 19 soybean varieties at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vienna_World_Exposition" title="Vienna World Exposition">Vienna World Exposition</a> (Wiener Weltausstellung). He cultivated these seeds in Vienna, and soon began to distribute them throughout Central and Western Europe. In 1875, he first grew the soybeans in Vienna, then in early 1876 he sent samples of seeds to seven cooperators in central Europe, who planted and tested the seeds in the spring of 1876, with good or fairly good results in each case.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shurtleff_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-shurtleff-118">[115]</a></sup> Most of the farmers who received seeds from him cultivated them, then reported their results. Starting in February 1876, he published these results first in various journal articles, and finally in his <i>magnum opus</i>, Die Sojabohne (The Soybean) in 1878.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shurtleff_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-shurtleff-118">[115]</a></sup> In northern Europe, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lupin" title="Lupin">lupin</a> (lupine) is known as the "soybean of the north".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[116]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Central_Asia">Central Asia</span></h3>
<p>The soybean is first in cultivated Transcaucasia in Central Asia in 1876, by the Dungans. This region has never been important for soybean production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[117]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Central_America">Central America</span></h3>
<p>The first reliable reference to the soybean in this region dates from Mexico in 1877.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-book-128_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-book-128-121">[118]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South_America">South America</span></h3>
<p>The soybean first arrived in South America in Argentina in 1882.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>Andrew McClung showed in the early 1950s that with soil amendments the <a href="/wiki/Cerrado" title="Cerrado">Cerrado</a> region of Brazil would grow soybeans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[120]</a></sup> In June 1973, when soybean futures markets mistakenly portended a major shortage, the <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Richard_Nixon" title="Presidency of Richard Nixon">Nixon administration</a> imposed an embargo on soybean exports. It lasted only a week, but Japanese buyers felt that they could not rely on U.S. supplies, and the rival Brazilian soybean industry came into existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-104">[101]</a></sup> This led Brazil to become the world's largest producer of soybeans in 2020, with 131 million tons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics">Genetics</span></h2>
<p>Chinese <a href="/wiki/Landrace" title="Landrace">landraces</a> were found to have a slightly higher diversity than inbred lines by Li et al. 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hinze-et-al-2017_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hinze-et-al-2017-126">[123]</a></sup> <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Specific_locus_amplified_fragment_sequencing&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Specific locus amplified fragment sequencing (page does not exist)">Specific locus amplified fragment sequencing</a> (<a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=SLAF-seq&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="SLAF-seq (page does not exist)">SLAF-seq</a>) has been used by Han et al. 2015 to study the genetic history of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crop_domestication" title="Crop domestication">domestication process</a>, perform <a href="/wiki/Genome-wide_association_study" title="Genome-wide association study">genome-wide association studies</a> (GWAS) of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agronomic_trait" title="Agronomic trait">agronomically relevant traits</a>, and produce <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-density_linkage_map" title="High-density linkage map">high-density linkage maps</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rasheed-et-al-2017_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rasheed-et-al-2017-127">[124]</a></sup> An <a href="/wiki/SNP_array" title="SNP array">SNP array</a> was developed by Song et al. 2013 and has been used for research and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crop_breeding" title="Crop breeding">breeding</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hulse-Kemp-et-al-2015_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hulse-Kemp-et-al-2015-128">[125]</a></sup> the same team applied their array in Song et al. 2015 against the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=USDA_Soybean_Germplasm_Collection&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="USDA Soybean Germplasm Collection (page does not exist)">USDA Soybean Germplasm Collection</a> and obtained mapping data that are expected to yield <a href="/wiki/Association_mapping" title="Association mapping">association mapping</a> data for such traits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hinze-et-al-2017_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hinze-et-al-2017-126">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetic_modification">Genetic modification</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_soybean" title="Genetically modified soybean">Genetically modified soybean</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Multicolor_soybeans_in_Hale_Township.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Multicolor_soybeans_in_Hale_Township.jpg/220px-Multicolor_soybeans_in_Hale_Township.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Multicolor_soybeans_in_Hale_Township.jpg/330px-Multicolor_soybeans_in_Hale_Township.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Multicolor_soybeans_in_Hale_Township.jpg/440px-Multicolor_soybeans_in_Hale_Township.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Multicolor_soybeans_in_Hale_Township.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Different varieties of soybeans being grown together</div></div></div>
<p>Soybeans are one of the "<a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">biotech</a> food" crops that have been <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_food" title="Genetically modified food">genetically modified</a>, and genetically modified soybeans are being used in an increasing number of products. In 1995, <a href="/wiki/Monsanto" title="Monsanto">Monsanto</a> company introduced <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_crops" title="Genetically modified crops">glyphosate-tolerant</a> soybeans that have been genetically modified to be resistant to Monsanto's <a href="/wiki/Glyphosate" title="Glyphosate">glyphosate</a> herbicides through substitution of the <i><a href="/wiki/Agrobacterium" title="Agrobacterium">Agrobacterium</a> sp.</i> (strain CP4) <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">gene</a> EPSP (5-enolpyruvyl shikimic acid-3-phosphate) synthase. The substituted version is not sensitive to <a href="/wiki/Glyphosate" title="Glyphosate">glyphosate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[126]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1997, about 8% of all soybeans cultivated for the commercial market in the United States were genetically modified. In 2010, the figure was 93%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[127]</a></sup> As with other glyphosate-tolerant crops, concern is expressed over damage to <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[128]</a></sup> A 2003 study<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[129]</a></sup> concluded the "Roundup Ready" (RR) gene had been bred into so many different soybean cultivars, there had been little decline in genetic diversity, but "diversity was limited among elite lines from some companies".
</p><p>The widespread use of such types of GM soybeans in the Americas has caused problems with exports to some regions. GM crops require extensive certification before they can be legally imported into the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, where there is considerable supplier and consumer reluctance to use GM products for consumer or animal use. Difficulties with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Co-existence_of_genetically_modified_and_conventional_crops_and_derived_food_and_feed" title="Co-existence of genetically modified and conventional crops and derived food and feed">coexistence</a> and subsequent traces of cross-contamination of non-GM stocks have caused shipments to be rejected and have put a premium on non-GM soy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2006 <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Agriculture" title="United States Department of Agriculture">United States Department of Agriculture</a> report found the adoption of genetically engineered (GE) soy, corn and cotton reduced the amount of pesticides used overall, but did result in a slightly greater amount of <a href="/wiki/Herbicide" title="Herbicide">herbicides</a> used for soy specifically. The use of GE soy was also associated with greater <a href="/wiki/No-till_farming" title="No-till farming">conservation tillage</a>, indirectly leading to better soil conservation, as well as increased income from off-farming sources due to the greater ease with which the crops can be managed. Though the overall estimated benefits of the adoption of GE soybeans in the United States was $310 million, the majority of this benefit was experienced by the companies selling the seeds (40%), followed by biotechnology firms (28%) and farmers (20%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[131]</a></sup> The patent on <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Glyphosate-tolerant&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Glyphosate-tolerant (page does not exist)">glyphosate-tolerant</a> soybeans expired in 2014,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[132]</a></sup> so benefits can be expected to shift.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2010, a team of American scientists announced they had sequenced the soybean genome—the first legume to be sequenced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nature-5297531_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nature-5297531-137">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[135]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses_2">Uses</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Global-soy-production-to-end-use.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1167" data-file-width="1618" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Global-soy-production-to-end-use.png/220px-Global-soy-production-to-end-use.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Global-soy-production-to-end-use.png/330px-Global-soy-production-to-end-use.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Global-soy-production-to-end-use.png/440px-Global-soy-production-to-end-use.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Global-soy-production-to-end-use.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Breakdown of what the world’s soy was used for in 2018</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TofuWithSoySauceAndCarrot.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="689" data-file-width="723" decoding="async" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/TofuWithSoySauceAndCarrot.jpg/220px-TofuWithSoySauceAndCarrot.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/TofuWithSoySauceAndCarrot.jpg/330px-TofuWithSoySauceAndCarrot.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/TofuWithSoySauceAndCarrot.jpg/440px-TofuWithSoySauceAndCarrot.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TofuWithSoySauceAndCarrot.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tofu and soy sauce</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-More_citations_needed_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Soybean&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">July 2021</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Among the legumes, the soybean is valued for its high (38–45%) <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> content as well as its high (approximately 20%) oil content. Soybeans are the most valuable agricultural export of the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[136]</a></sup> Approximately 85% of the world's soybean crop is processed into soybean meal and soybean oil, the remainder processed in other ways or eaten whole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[137]</a></sup>
</p><p>Soybeans can be broadly classified as "vegetable" (garden) or field (oil) types. Vegetable types cook more easily, have a mild, nutty flavor, better texture, are larger in size, higher in protein, and lower in oil than field types. <a href="/wiki/Tofu" title="Tofu">Tofu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soy_milk" title="Soy milk">soy milk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soy_sauce" title="Soy sauce">soy sauce</a> are among the top edible commodities made using soybeans. Producers prefer the higher protein cultivars bred from vegetable soybeans originally brought to the United States in the late 1930s. The "garden" cultivars are generally not suitable for mechanical combine harvesting because there is a tendency for the pods to shatter upon reaching maturity.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Soybean_oil">Soybean oil</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Soybean_oil" title="Soybean oil">Soybean oil</a></div>
<p>Soybean seed contains 18–19% oil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-141">[138]</a></sup> To extract soybean oil from seed, the soybeans are cracked, adjusted for moisture content, rolled into flakes and solvent-extracted with commercial hexane. The oil is then refined, blended for different applications, and sometimes hydrogenated. Soybean oils, both liquid and partially hydrogenated, are exported abroad, sold as "vegetable oil", or end up in a wide variety of processed foods.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Soybean_meal">Soybean meal</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Soybean_meal" title="Soybean meal">Soybean meal</a></div>
<p>Soybean meal, or soymeal, is the material remaining after solvent extraction of oil from soybean flakes, with a 50% <a href="/wiki/Soy_protein" title="Soy protein">soy protein</a> content. The meal is 'toasted' (a <a href="/wiki/Misnomer" title="Misnomer">misnomer</a> because the heat treatment is with moist steam) and ground in a <a href="/wiki/Hammer_mill" title="Hammer mill">hammer mill</a>. Ninety-seven percent of soybean meal production globally is used as livestock feed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-141">[138]</a></sup> Soybean meal is also used in some <a href="/wiki/Dog_food" title="Dog food">dog foods</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lusas_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lusas-142">[139]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Livestock_feed">Livestock feed</span></h3>
<p>One of the major uses of soybeans globally is as livestock feed, predominantly in the form of soybean meal. In the European Union, for example, though it does not make up most of the weight of livestock <a href="/wiki/Animal_feed" title="Animal feed">feed</a>, soybean meal provides around 60% of the protein fed to livestock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[140]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vernal_grass" title="Vernal grass">Spring grasses</a> are rich in omega-3 fatty acids, whereas soy is predominantly omega-6. The soybean hulls, which mainly consist of the outer coats of the beans removed before oil extraction, can also be fed to livestock, as well as whole soybean seeds after processing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[142]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Food_for_human_consumption">Food for human consumption</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tempeh_003.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4288" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Tempeh_003.jpg/220px-Tempeh_003.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Tempeh_003.jpg/330px-Tempeh_003.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Tempeh_003.jpg/440px-Tempeh_003.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tempeh_003.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Tempeh" title="Tempeh">Tempeh</a>, fermented soybean cake</div></div></div>
<p>In addition to their use in livestock feed, soybean products are widely used for human consumption. Common soybean products include <a href="/wiki/Soy_sauce" title="Soy sauce">soy sauce</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soy_milk" title="Soy milk">soy milk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tofu" title="Tofu">tofu</a>, <a href="#Meal">soy meal</a>, <a href="#Flour">soy flour</a>, <a href="/wiki/Textured_vegetable_protein" title="Textured vegetable protein">textured vegetable protein</a> (TVP), <a href="/wiki/Soy_curls" title="Soy curls">soy curls</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tempeh" title="Tempeh">tempeh</a>, soy <a href="/wiki/Lecithin" title="Lecithin">lecithin</a> and <a href="#Oil">soybean oil</a>. Soybeans may also be eaten with minimal processing, for example in the Japanese food <i><a href="/wiki/Edamame" title="Edamame">edamame</a></i><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">枝豆</span></span>, <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">edamame</i></span>)</span>, in which immature soybeans are boiled whole in their pods and served with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edible_salt" title="Edible salt">salt</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aesthetic_meal_maker_of_Salem.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2368" data-file-width="4208" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Aesthetic_meal_maker_of_Salem.jpg/220px-Aesthetic_meal_maker_of_Salem.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Aesthetic_meal_maker_of_Salem.jpg/330px-Aesthetic_meal_maker_of_Salem.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Aesthetic_meal_maker_of_Salem.jpg/440px-Aesthetic_meal_maker_of_Salem.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aesthetic_meal_maker_of_Salem.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Soy chunks</div></div></div>
<p>In China, Japan, and <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a>, soybean and soybean products are a common part of the diet. <a href="/wiki/Tofu" title="Tofu">Tofu</a> (豆腐 <i>dòufu</i>) is thought to have originated in China, along with <a href="/wiki/Soy_sauce" title="Soy sauce">soy sauce</a> and several varieties of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soybean_paste" title="Soybean paste">soybean paste</a> used as seasonings.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Japanese foods made from soya include <i><a href="/wiki/Miso" title="Miso">miso</a></i> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">味噌</span></span><span class="lang-comment" style="font-style: normal; display: none; color: #33aa33; margin-left: 0.3em;">code: jpn promoted to code: ja </span>), <i><a href="/wiki/Natt%C5%8D" title="Nattō">nattō</a></i> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">納豆</span></span><span class="lang-comment" style="font-style: normal; display: none; color: #33aa33; margin-left: 0.3em;">code: jpn promoted to code: ja </span>), <i><a href="/wiki/Kinako" title="Kinako">kinako</a></i> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">黄粉</span></span><span class="lang-comment" style="font-style: normal; display: none; color: #33aa33; margin-left: 0.3em;">code: jpn promoted to code: ja </span>) and <i>edamame</i> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">枝豆</span></span><span class="lang-comment" style="font-style: normal; display: none; color: #33aa33; margin-left: 0.3em;">code: jpn promoted to code: ja </span>), as well as products made with tofu such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atsuage" title="Atsuage">atsuage</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aburaage" title="Aburaage">aburaage</a>. In China, whole dried soybeans are sold in supermarkets and used to cook a variety of dishes, usually after rehydration by soaking in water; they find their use in soup or as a savory dish. In <a href="/wiki/Korean_cuisine" title="Korean cuisine">Korean cuisine</a>, soybean sprouts (<a class="extiw" href="https://ko.wikipedia.org/wiki/%EC%BD%A9%EB%82%98%EB%AC%BC" title="ko:콩나물">콩나물</a> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kongnamul" title="Kongnamul">kongnamul</a></i>) are used in a variety of dishes, and soybeans are the base ingredient in <i><a href="/wiki/Doenjang" title="Doenjang">doenjang</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Cheonggukjang" title="Cheonggukjang">cheonggukjang</a></i> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kanjang" title="Kanjang"><i>ganjang</i></a>. In Vietnam, soybeans are used to make soybean paste (<i><a href="/wiki/T%C6%B0%C6%A1ng" title="Tương">tương</a></i>) in the North with the most popular products are <i>tương Bần</i>, <i>tương Nam Đàn</i>, <i>tương Cự Đà</i> as a garnish for <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ph%E1%BB%9F" title="Phở">phở</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/G%E1%BB%8Fi_cu%E1%BB%91n" title="Gỏi cuốn">gỏi cuốn</a></i> dishes, as well as tofu (<span title="Vietnamese-language text"><i lang="vi">đậu hũ</i></span><span class="lang-comment" style="font-style: normal; display: none; color: #33aa33; margin-left: 0.3em;">code: vie promoted to code: vi </span> or <span title="Vietnamese-language text"><i lang="vi">đậu phụ</i></span><span class="lang-comment" style="font-style: normal; display: none; color: #33aa33; margin-left: 0.3em;">code: vie promoted to code: vi </span> or <span title="Vietnamese-language text"><i lang="vi">tàu hũ</i></span><span class="lang-comment" style="font-style: normal; display: none; color: #33aa33; margin-left: 0.3em;">code: vie promoted to code: vi </span>), soy sauce (<span title="Vietnamese-language text"><i lang="vi">nước tương</i></span><span class="lang-comment" style="font-style: normal; display: none; color: #33aa33; margin-left: 0.3em;">code: vie promoted to code: vi </span>), soy milk (<span title="Vietnamese-language text"><i lang="vi">nước đậu</i></span><span class="lang-comment" style="font-style: normal; display: none; color: #33aa33; margin-left: 0.3em;">code: vie promoted to code: vi </span> in the North or <span title="Vietnamese-language text"><i lang="vi">sữa đậu nành</i></span><span class="lang-comment" style="font-style: normal; display: none; color: #33aa33; margin-left: 0.3em;">code: vie promoted to code: vi </span> in the South), and <span title="Vietnamese-language text"><i lang="vi">đậu hũ nước đường</i></span><span class="lang-comment" style="font-style: normal; display: none; color: #33aa33; margin-left: 0.3em;">code: vie promoted to code: vi </span> (tofu sweet soup).
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Flour">Flour</span></h4>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097025294">.mw-parser-output .side-box{margin:4px 0;box-sizing:border-box;border:1px solid #aaa;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em;background-color:#f9f9f9}.mw-parser-output .side-box-abovebelow,.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{padding:0.25em 0.9em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-image{padding:2px 0 2px 0.9em;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-imageright{padding:2px 0.9em 2px 0;text-align:center}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .side-box-flex{display:flex;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{flex:1}}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .side-box{width:238px}.mw-parser-output .side-box-right{clear:right;float:right;margin-left:1em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-left{margin-right:1em}}</style><div class="side-box side-box-right plainlinks sistersitebox">
<div class="side-box-flex">
<div class="side-box-image"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="1376" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/30px-Commons-logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/45px-Commons-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/59px-Commons-logo.svg.png 2x" width="30"/></div>
<div class="side-box-text plainlist">Wikimedia Commons has media related to <span style="font-weight: bold; font-style: italic;"><a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Category:Soy_powder" title="commons:Category:Soy powder">Soybean flour</a></span>.</div></div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:JapaneseSoybeanMeat.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1474" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/JapaneseSoybeanMeat.jpg/220px-JapaneseSoybeanMeat.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/JapaneseSoybeanMeat.jpg/330px-JapaneseSoybeanMeat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/JapaneseSoybeanMeat.jpg/440px-JapaneseSoybeanMeat.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:JapaneseSoybeanMeat.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Japanese soybean meat</div></div></div>
<p>Soy flour refers to soybeans ground finely enough to pass through a 100-mesh or smaller screen where special care was taken during desolventizing (not toasted) to minimize <a href="/wiki/Denaturation_(biochemistry)" title="Denaturation (biochemistry)">denaturation</a> of the protein to retain a high <a href="/wiki/Protein_dispersibility_index" title="Protein dispersibility index">protein dispersibility index</a>, for uses such as <a href="/wiki/Food_extrusion" title="Food extrusion">food extrusion</a> of <a href="/wiki/Textured_vegetable_protein" title="Textured vegetable protein">textured vegetable protein</a>. It is the starting material for production of soy concentrate and soy protein isolate.
</p><p>Soy flour can also be made by roasting the soybean, removing the coat (hull), and grinding into a flour. Soy flour is manufactured with different fat levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELim2012637_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELim2012637-146">[143]</a></sup> Alternatively, <b>raw</b> soy flour omits the roasting step.
</p>
<ul><li>Defatted soy flour is obtained from <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvent</a> <a href="/wiki/Extraction_(chemistry)" title="Extraction (chemistry)">extracted</a> flakes, and contains less than 1% oil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELim2012637_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELim2012637-146">[143]</a></sup></li>
<li>"Natural or full-fat soy flour is made from unextracted, dehulled beans, and contains about 18% to 20% oil."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELim2012637_146-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELim2012637-146">[143]</a></sup> Its high oil content requires the use of a specialized Alpine Fine Impact Mill to grind rather than the usual <a href="/wiki/Hammer_mill" title="Hammer mill">hammer mill</a>. Full-fat soy flour has a lower protein concentration than defatted flour. Extruded Full-Fat soy flour, ground in an Alpine mill, can replace/extend EGGS in baking and cooking<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[145]</a></sup> Full-fat soy flour is a component of the famous <a href="/wiki/Cornell_bread" title="Cornell bread">Cornell bread</a> recipe (think pizza)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[148]</a></sup></li>
<li>Low-fat soy flour is made by adding some oil back into defatted soy flour. Fat levels range from 4.5% to 9%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELim2012637_146-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELim2012637-146">[143]</a></sup></li>
<li>High-fat soy flour can also be produced by adding back soybean oil to defatted flour, usually at the level of 15%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[149]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soy_lecithin" title="Soy lecithin">Soy lecithin</a> can be added (up to 15%) to soy flour to make lecithinated soy flour. It increases dispersibility and gives it emulsifying properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELim2012637_146-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELim2012637-146">[143]</a></sup>
</p><p>Soy flour has 50% protein and 5% fiber. It has higher levels of protein, thiamine, riboflavin, phosphorus, calcium, and iron than <a href="/wiki/Wheat_flour" title="Wheat flour">wheat flour</a>. It does not contain <a href="/wiki/Gluten" title="Gluten">gluten</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELim2012637_146-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELim2012637-146">[143]</a></sup> As a result, <a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">yeast</a>-raised <a href="/wiki/Bread" title="Bread">breads</a> made with soy flour are dense in texture. Among many uses, soy flour thickens sauces, prevents <a href="/wiki/Staling" title="Staling">staling</a> in baked food, and reduces oil absorption during frying. Baking food with soy flour gives it tenderness, moistness, a rich color, and a fine texture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELim2012637_146-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELim2012637-146">[143]</a></sup>
</p><p>Soy grits are similar to soy flour except the soybeans have been toasted and cracked into coarse pieces.
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Kinako" title="Kinako">Kinako</a></i> is a soy flour used in <a href="/wiki/Japanese_cuisine" title="Japanese cuisine">Japanese cuisine</a>.
</p><p><span style="font-size:90%;"><i>Section reference</i>: <a href="#CITEREFSmithCircle1972">Smith &amp; Circle (1972</a>, p. 442)<span class="error harv-error" style="display: none; font-size:100%"> harvtxt error: no target: CITEREFSmithCircle1972 (<a href="/wiki/Category:Harv_and_Sfn_template_errors" title="Category:Harv and Sfn template errors">help</a>)</span></span>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Soy-based_infant_formula">Soy-based infant formula<span class="anchor" id="Soy-based_infant_formula"></span></span></h4>
<p>Soy-based <a href="/wiki/Infant_formula" title="Infant formula">infant formula</a> (SBIF) is sometimes given to infants who are not being strictly breastfed; it can be useful for infants who are either allergic to pasteurized cow milk proteins or who are being fed a <a href="/wiki/Veganism" title="Veganism">vegan</a> diet. It is sold in powdered, ready-to-feed, and concentrated liquid forms.
</p><p>Some reviews have expressed the opinion that more research is needed to determine what effect the phytoestrogens in soybeans may have on infants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[150]</a></sup> Diverse studies have concluded there are no adverse effects in human growth, development, or reproduction as a result of the consumption of soy-based infant formula.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nutri_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nutri-156">[153]</a></sup> One of these studies, published in the <i><a href="/wiki/Journal_of_Nutrition" title="Journal of Nutrition">Journal of Nutrition</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nutri_156-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nutri-156">[153]</a></sup> concludes that there are:
</p>
<blockquote><p>... no clinical concerns with respect to nutritional adequacy, sexual development, neurobehavioral development, immune development, or thyroid disease. SBIFs provide complete nutrition that adequately supports normal infant growth and development. FDA has accepted SBIFs as safe for use as the sole source of nutrition.</p></blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Meat_and_dairy_alternatives_and_extenders">Meat and dairy alternatives and extenders</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chives_Cream_Sheese.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="637" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Chives_Cream_Sheese.jpg/220px-Chives_Cream_Sheese.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Chives_Cream_Sheese.jpg/330px-Chives_Cream_Sheese.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Chives_Cream_Sheese.jpg/440px-Chives_Cream_Sheese.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chives_Cream_Sheese.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Package of a soy-based cream cheese alternative with chives</div></div></div>
<p>Soybeans can be processed to produce a texture and appearance similar to many other foods. For example, soybeans are the primary ingredient in many <a href="/wiki/Dairy_product" title="Dairy product">dairy product</a> substitutes (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Soy_milk" title="Soy milk">soy milk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Margarine" title="Margarine">margarine</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Soy_ice_cream&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Soy ice cream (page does not exist)">soy ice cream</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soy_yogurt" title="Soy yogurt">soy yogurt</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soy_cheese" title="Soy cheese">soy cheese</a>, and soy cream cheese) and meat alternatives (e.g. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Veggie_burgers" title="Veggie burgers">veggie burgers</a>). These substitutes are readily available in most supermarkets. Soy milk does not naturally contain significant amounts of digestible <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a>. Many manufacturers of soy milk sell calcium-enriched products, as well.
</p><p>Soy products also are used as a low-cost substitute in meat and poultry products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[155]</a></sup> Food service, retail and institutional (primarily school lunch and correctional) facilities regularly use such "extended" products. Extension may result in diminished flavor, but fat and cholesterol are reduced. Vitamin and mineral fortification can be used to make soy products nutritionally equivalent to animal protein; the protein quality is already roughly equivalent. The soy-based meat substitute <a href="/wiki/Textured_vegetable_protein" title="Textured vegetable protein">textured vegetable protein</a> has been used for more than 50 years as a way of inexpensively extending <a href="/wiki/Ground_beef" title="Ground beef">ground beef</a> without reducing its nutritional value.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Riaz2006_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Riaz2006-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[157]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Soy_nut_butter">Soy nut butter</span></h4>
<p>The soybean is used to make a product called soy <a href="/wiki/Nut_butter" title="Nut butter">nut butter</a> which is similar in texture to peanut butter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[158]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sweetened_soybean">Sweetened soybean</span></h4>
<p>Sweet boiled beans are popular in Japan and Korea and the sweet boiled soybeans are called as "Daizu no <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Nimame&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Nimame (page does not exist)">Nimame</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://ja.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E7%85%AE%E8%B1%86" title="ja:煮豆">ja</a>]</span>" in Japan and Kongjorim (<a href="/wiki/Korean_language" title="Korean language">Korean</a>: <span lang="ko">콩조림</span>) in Korea. Sweet boiled beans are even used in sweetened buns, especially in <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Mame_Pan&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Mame Pan (page does not exist)">Mame Pan</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://ja.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E8%B1%86%E3%83%91%E3%83%B3" title="ja:豆パン">ja</a>]</span>.
</p><p>The boiled and pasted edamame, called <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Zunda&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Zunda (page does not exist)">Zunda</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://ja.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E3%81%9A%E3%82%93%E3%81%A0" title="ja:ずんだ">ja</a>]</span>, is used as one of the <a href="/wiki/Sweet_bean_paste" title="Sweet bean paste">Sweet bean pastes</a> in <a href="/wiki/Wagashi" title="Wagashi">Japanese confections</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Coffee_substitute">Coffee substitute</span></h4>
<p>Roasted and ground soybeans can be used as a caffeine-free substitute for <a href="/wiki/Coffee" title="Coffee">coffee</a>. After the soybeans are roasted and ground, they look similar to regular coffee beans or can be used as a powder similar to instant coffee, with aroma and flavor of roasted soybeans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[159]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_products">Other products</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Display_of_soybean_wax_candle_in_Texas_store.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4032" data-file-width="3024" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Display_of_soybean_wax_candle_in_Texas_store.jpg/220px-Display_of_soybean_wax_candle_in_Texas_store.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Display_of_soybean_wax_candle_in_Texas_store.jpg/330px-Display_of_soybean_wax_candle_in_Texas_store.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Display_of_soybean_wax_candle_in_Texas_store.jpg/440px-Display_of_soybean_wax_candle_in_Texas_store.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Display_of_soybean_wax_candle_in_Texas_store.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Display of soybean wax candle in Texas store</div></div></div><p>Soybeans with black hulls are used in Chinese fermented black beans, <i><a href="/wiki/Douchi" title="Douchi">douchi</a></i>, not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Black_turtle_bean" title="Black turtle bean">black turtle beans</a>.
</p><p>Soybeans are also used in industrial products, including oils, soap, cosmetics, <a href="/wiki/Resin" title="Resin">resins</a>, plastics, inks, crayons, <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvents</a>, and clothing. Soybean oil is the primary source of <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">biodiesel</a> in the United States, accounting for 80% of domestic biodiesel production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[160]</a></sup> Soybeans have also been used since 2001 as fermenting stock in the manufacture of a brand of <a href="/wiki/Vodka" title="Vodka">vodka</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[161]</a></sup> In 1936, Ford Motor Company developed a method where soybeans and fibers were rolled together producing a soup which was then pressed into various parts for their cars, from the distributor cap to knobs on the dash board. Ford also informed in public relation releases that in 1935 over five million acres (20,000 km<sup>2</sup>) was dedicated to growing soybeans in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PM_Apr_1936_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-PM_Apr_1936-165">[162]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_effects">Health effects</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cancer">Cancer</span></h3>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/American_Cancer_Society" title="American Cancer Society">American Cancer Society</a>, "There is growing evidence that eating traditional soy foods such as tofu may lower the risk of cancers of the breast, prostate, or endometrium (lining of the uterus), and there is some evidence it may lower the risk of certain other cancers." There is insufficient research to indicate whether taking soy <a href="/wiki/Dietary_supplement" title="Dietary supplement">dietary supplements</a> (e.g., as a pill or capsule) has any effect on health or cancer risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[163]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2018, rigorous dietary <a href="/wiki/Clinical_research" title="Clinical research">clinical research</a> in people with cancer has proved inconclusive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-lpi-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yu_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yu-167">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tse_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tse-168">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acs_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-acs-169">[166]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vandie_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-vandie-170">[167]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Breast_cancer">Breast cancer</span></h4>
<p>Although considerable research has examined the potential for soy consumption to lower the risk of <a href="/wiki/Breast_cancer" title="Breast cancer">breast cancer</a> in women, as of 2016 there is insufficient evidence to reach a conclusion about a relationship between soy consumption and any effects on breast cancer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-lpi-42">[42]</a></sup> A 2011 <a href="/wiki/Meta-analysis" title="Meta-analysis">meta-analysis</a> stated: "Our study suggests soy isoflavones intake is associated with a significant reduced risk of breast cancer incidence in Asian populations, but not in Western populations."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[168]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gastrointestinal_and_colorectal_cancer">Gastrointestinal and colorectal cancer</span></h4>
<p>Reviews of preliminary clinical trials on people with <a href="/wiki/Colorectal_cancer" title="Colorectal cancer">colorectal</a> or <a href="/wiki/Gastrointestinal_cancer" title="Gastrointestinal cancer">gastrointestinal cancer</a> suggest that soy isoflavones may have a slight protective effect against such cancers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yu_167-1"><a href="#cite_note-Yu-167">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tse_168-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tse-168">[165]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Prostate_cancer">Prostate cancer</span></h4>
<p>A 2016 review concluded that "current evidence from <a href="/wiki/Observational_study" title="Observational study">observational studies</a> and small clinical trials is not robust enough to understand whether soy protein or isoflavone supplements may help prevent or inhibit the progression of prostate cancer."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_42-3"><a href="#cite_note-lpi-42">[42]</a></sup> A 2010 review showed that neither soy foods nor isoflavone supplements alter measures of bioavailable <a href="/wiki/Testosterone" title="Testosterone">testosterone</a> or <a href="/wiki/Estrogen" title="Estrogen">estrogen</a> concentrations in men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[169]</a></sup> Soy consumption has been shown to have no effect on the levels and quality of <a href="/wiki/Sperm" title="Sperm">sperm</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[170]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Meta-analysis" title="Meta-analysis">Meta-analyses</a> on the association between soy consumption and prostate cancer risk in men concluded that dietary soy may lower the risk of prostate cancer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vandie_170-1"><a href="#cite_note-vandie-170">[167]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cardiovascular_health">Cardiovascular health</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">Food and Drug Administration</a> (FDA) granted the following health claim for soy: "25 grams of soy protein a day, as part of a diet low in saturated fat and cholesterol, may reduce the risk of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heart_disease" title="Heart disease">heart disease</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fda-chd_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-fda-chd-26">[26]</a></sup> One serving, (1 cup or 240 mL) of soy milk, for instance, contains 6 or 7 grams of soy protein.
</p><p>An <a href="/wiki/American_Heart_Association" title="American Heart Association">American Heart Association</a> (AHA) review of a decade long study of soy protein benefits did not recommend isoflavone supplementation. The review panel also found that soy isoflavones have not been shown to reduce post-menopausal "hot flashes" and the efficacy and safety of isoflavones to help prevent cancers of the breast, uterus or prostate is in question. AHA concluded that "many soy products should be beneficial to cardiovascular and overall health because of their high content of polyunsaturated fats, fiber, vitamins, and minerals and low content of saturated fat".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cardio_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-cardio-175">[172]</a></sup> Other studies found that soy protein consumption could lower <a href="/wiki/Low-density_lipoprotein" title="Low-density lipoprotein">LDL</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[174]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Soy_allergy">Soy allergy</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Soy_allergy" title="Soy allergy">Soy allergy</a></div>
<p>Allergy to soy is common, and the food is listed with other foods that commonly cause allergy, such as milk, eggs, peanuts, tree nuts, shellfish. The problem has been reported among younger children, and the diagnosis of soy allergy is often based on symptoms reported by parents and results of skin tests or blood tests for allergy. Only a few reported studies have attempted to confirm allergy to soy by direct challenge with the food under controlled conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[175]</a></sup> It is very difficult to give a reliable estimate of the true prevalence of soy allergy in the general population. To the extent that it does exist, soy allergy may cause cases of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urticaria" title="Urticaria">urticaria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Angioedema" title="Angioedema">angioedema</a>, usually within minutes to hours of ingestion. In rare cases, true <a href="/wiki/Anaphylaxis" title="Anaphylaxis">anaphylaxis</a> may also occur. The reason for the discrepancy is likely that soy proteins, the causative factor in <a href="/wiki/Allergy" title="Allergy">allergy</a>, are far less potent at triggering allergy symptoms than the proteins of peanut and shellfish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[176]</a></sup> An allergy test that is positive demonstrates that the immune system has formed IgE antibodies to soy proteins. However, this is only a factor when soy proteins reach the blood without being digested, in sufficient quantities to reach a threshold to provoke actual symptoms.
</p><p>Soy can also trigger symptoms via <a href="/wiki/Food_intolerance" title="Food intolerance">food intolerance</a>, a situation where no allergic mechanism can be proven. One scenario is seen in very young infants who have vomiting and <a href="/wiki/Diarrhea" title="Diarrhea">diarrhoea</a> when fed soy-based formula, which resolves when the formula is withdrawn. Older infants can suffer a more severe disorder with vomiting, diarrhoea that may be bloody, <a href="/wiki/Anemia" title="Anemia">anemia</a>, weight loss and failure to thrive. The most common cause of this unusual disorder is a sensitivity to cow's milk, but soy formulas can also be the trigger. The precise mechanism is unclear and it could be immunologic, although not through the IgE-type antibodies that have the leading role in urticaria and anaphylaxis. However, it is also self-limiting and will often disappear in the <a href="/wiki/Toddler" title="Toddler">toddler</a> years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[177]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, identifying the presence of soy either as an ingredient or unintended contaminant in packaged food is compulsory. The regulation (EC) 1169/2011 on food-labeling lists 14 allergens, including soy, in packaged food must be clearly indicated on the label as part of the list of ingredients, using a distinctive typography (such as bold type or capital letters).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[178]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Thyroid_function">Thyroid function</span></h3>
<p>One review noted that soy-based foods may inhibit absorption of thyroid hormone medications required for treatment of <a href="/wiki/Hypothyroidism" title="Hypothyroidism">hypothyroidism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[179]</a></sup> A 2015 scientific review by the <a href="/wiki/European_Food_Safety_Authority" title="European Food Safety Authority">European Food Safety Authority</a> concluded that intake of isoflavones from supplements did not affect thyroid hormone levels in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Postmenopausal" title="Postmenopausal">postmenopausal</a> women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[180]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Research_by_constituent">Research by constituent</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Lignans">Lignans</span></h4>
<p>Plant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lignans" title="Lignans">lignans</a> are associated with high fiber foods such as cereal brans and beans are the principal precursor to mammalian lignans which have an ability to bind to human estrogen sites. Soybeans are a significant source of mammalian lignan precursor <a href="/wiki/Secoisolariciresinol" title="Secoisolariciresinol">secoisolariciresinol</a> containing 13–273 µg/100 g dry weight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[181]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Phytochemicals">Phytochemicals</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phytochemicals" title="Phytochemicals">Phytochemicals</a></div>
<p>Soybeans and processed soy foods are among the richest foods in total <a href="/wiki/Phytoestrogen" title="Phytoestrogen">phytoestrogens</a> (wet basis per 100 g), which are present primarily in the form of the <a href="/wiki/Isoflavone" title="Isoflavone">isoflavones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daidzein" title="Daidzein">daidzein</a> and <a href="/wiki/Genistein" title="Genistein">genistein</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_42-4"><a href="#cite_note-lpi-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[182]</a></sup> Because most naturally occurring phytoestrogens act as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Selective_estrogen_receptor_modulators" title="Selective estrogen receptor modulators">selective estrogen receptor modulators</a>, or SERMs, which do not necessarily act as direct agonists of estrogen receptors, normal consumption of foods that contain these phytoestrogens should not provide sufficient amounts to elicit a physiological response in humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11352776_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid11352776-186">[183]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[184]</a></sup> The major product of daidzein microbial metabolism is <a href="/wiki/Equol" title="Equol">equol</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid30614249_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid30614249-188">[185]</a></sup> Only 33% of Western Europeans have a <a href="/wiki/Microbiome" title="Microbiome">microbiome</a> that produces equol, compared to 50–55% of Asians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid30614249_188-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid30614249-188">[185]</a></sup>
</p><p>Soy isoflavones—<a href="/wiki/Polyphenol" title="Polyphenol">polyphenolic</a> compounds that are also produced by other legumes like peanuts and <a href="/wiki/Chickpea" title="Chickpea">chickpeas</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_42-5"><a href="#cite_note-lpi-42">[42]</a></sup>—are under preliminary research. As of 2016, no <a href="/wiki/Causality" title="Causality">cause-and-effect</a> relationship has been shown in <a href="/wiki/Clinical_research" title="Clinical research">clinical research</a> to indicate that soy isoflavones lower the risk of cardiovascular diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_42-6"><a href="#cite_note-lpi-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cardio_175-1"><a href="#cite_note-cardio-175">[172]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[186]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Phytic_acid">Phytic acid</span></h4>
<p>Soybeans contain <a href="/wiki/Phytic_acid" title="Phytic acid">phytic acid</a>, which may act as a <a href="/wiki/Chelation" title="Chelation">chelating agent</a> and inhibit mineral absorption, especially for diets already low in minerals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[187]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_culture">In culture</span></h2>
<p>Although observations of soy consumption having a feminization effect on men<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[188]</a></sup> are not conclusive,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[189]</a></sup> a pejorative term, "<a href="/wiki/Soy_boy" title="Soy boy">soy boy</a>", has emerged to describe perceived emasculated young men with <a href="/wiki/Femininity" title="Femininity">feminine</a> traits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[190]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Futures">Futures</span></h2>
<p>Soybean <a href="/wiki/Futures_contract" title="Futures contract">futures</a> are traded on the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Board_of_Trade" title="Chicago Board of Trade">Chicago Board of Trade</a> and have delivery dates in January (F), March (H), May (K), July (N), August (Q), September (U), November (X).
</p><p>They are also traded on other commodity futures exchanges under different contract specifications:
</p>
<ul><li>SAFEX: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_South_African_Futures_Exchange" title="The South African Futures Exchange">The South African Futures Exchange</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[191]</a></sup></li>
<li>DC: <a href="/wiki/Dalian_Commodity_Exchange" title="Dalian Commodity Exchange">Dalian Commodity Exchange</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[192]</a></sup></li>
<li>ODE: <a href="/wiki/Osaka_Dojima_Commodity_Exchange" title="Osaka Dojima Commodity Exchange">Osaka Dojima Commodity Exchange</a> (formerly Kansai Commodities Exchange, KEX) in Japan<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[193]</a></sup></li>
<li>NCDEX: National Commodity and Derivatives Exchange, India.</li>
<li>ROFEX: Rosario Grain Exchange in Argentina</li></ul>

Retrieved <span class="nowrap">February 19,</span> 2012</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=%E4%BA%A4%E6%98%93%E6%89%80%E5%8A%A8%E6%80%81&amp;rft.pub=Dalian+Commodity+Exchange&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.dce.com.cn&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASoybean"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-196"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-196">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="http://ode.or.jp/english/index.html" rel="nofollow">"Exchange Introduction"</a>. <i>Osaka Dojima Commodity Exchange</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. </li>

<ul><li><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation magazine cs1" id="CITEREFGullickson2012">Gullickson, Gill (October 1, 2012). <a class="external text" href="http://www.agriculture.com/crops/soybeans/production/7-steps-to-better-soybe-yields_141-ar26627" rel="nofollow">"7 STEPS TO BETTER SOYBEAN YIELDS"</a>. <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Successful_Farming" title="Successful Farming">Successful Farming</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Meredith_Corporation" title="Meredith Corporation">Meredith Corporation</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Successful+Farming&amp;rft.atitle=7+STEPS+TO+BETTER+SOYBEAN+YIELDS&amp;rft.date=2012-10-01&amp;rft.aulast=Gullickson&amp;rft.aufirst=Gill&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.agriculture.com%2Fcrops%2Fsoybeans%2Fproduction%2F7-steps-to-better-soybe-yields_141-ar26627&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASoybean"></span></li></ul>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1011085734" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="reflist">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-Tylka-40-sympt-58"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Tylka-40-sympt_58-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">“You can literally have 40% yield loss with no symptoms,” says Greg Tylka, Iowa State University (ISU) Extension nematologist.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-PI-88788-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-PI-88788_63-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Reliance on the main genetic source of SCN resistance (PI 88788)may be helping SCN to overcome SCN-resistant varieties. Out of 807 resistant varieties listed by ISU this year, just 18 had a genetic background outside of PI 88788. “We have lots of varieties to pick from, but the genetic background is not as diverse as we would like it to be,” says Tylka.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-overcome-resis-67"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-overcome-resis_67-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">There have been cases where SCN has clipped yields of SCN-resistant varieties. Reliance on the main genetic source of SCN resistance (PI 88788)may be helping SCN to overcome SCN-resistant varieties.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
